Title: Ontology

URL Source: https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ontology

Published Time: 2001-09-26T01:23:44Z

Markdown Content:
This article is about the philosophical study of being. For the information science concept, see [Ontology (information science)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ontology_(information_science) "Ontology (information science)").

**Ontology** is the philosophical study of [being](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Existence "Existence"). It is traditionally understood as the subdiscipline of [metaphysics](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Metaphysics "Metaphysics") focused on the most general features of [reality](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Reality "Reality"). As one of the most fundamental concepts, being encompasses all of reality and every [entity](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Entity "Entity") within it. To articulate the basic structure of being, ontology examines the commonalities among all things and investigates their classification into basic types, such as the [categories](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Theory_of_categories "Theory of categories") of [particulars](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Particular "Particular") and [universals](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Universal_(metaphysics) "Universal (metaphysics)"). Particulars are unique, non-repeatable entities, such as the person [Socrates](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Socrates "Socrates"), whereas universals are general, repeatable entities, like the color _green_. Another distinction exists between [concrete](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abstract_and_concrete "Abstract and concrete") objects existing in [space and time](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Space_and_time "Space and time"), such as a tree, and abstract objects existing outside space and time, like the number 7. Systems of categories aim to provide a comprehensive inventory of reality by employing categories such as [substance](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Substance_theory "Substance theory"), [property](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Property_(philosophy) "Property (philosophy)"), [relation](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Relation_(philosophy) "Relation (philosophy)"), [state of affairs](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/State_of_affairs_(philosophy) "State of affairs (philosophy)"), and [event](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Event_(philosophy) "Event (philosophy)").

Ontologists disagree regarding which entities exist at the most basic level. [Platonic realism](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Platonic_realism "Platonic realism") asserts that universals have objective existence, while [conceptualism](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Conceptualism "Conceptualism") maintains that universals exist only in the mind, and [nominalism](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nominalism "Nominalism") denies their existence altogether. Similar disputes pertain to [mathematical objects](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mathematical_object "Mathematical object"), [unobservable](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Unobservable "Unobservable") objects assumed by scientific theories, and [moral facts](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ethics#Realism,_relativism,_and_nihilism "Ethics"). [Materialism](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Materialism "Materialism") posits that fundamentally only [matter](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Matter "Matter") exists, whereas [dualism](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mind%E2%80%93body_dualism "Mind–body dualism") asserts that [mind](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mind "Mind") and matter are independent principles. According to some ontologists, objective answers to ontological questions do not exist, with perspectives shaped by differing linguistic practices.

Ontology employs diverse [methods of inquiry](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Philosophical_methodology "Philosophical methodology"), including the analysis of [concepts](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Philosophical_analysis "Philosophical analysis") and [experience](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Phenomenology_(philosophy) "Phenomenology (philosophy)"), the use of [intuitions](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Intuition "Intuition") and [thought experiments](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Thought_experiment "Thought experiment"), and the integration of findings from [natural science](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Natural_science "Natural science"). [Formal ontology](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Formal_ontology "Formal ontology") investigates the most abstract features of objects, while [Applied ontology](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Applied_ontology "Applied ontology") utilizes ontological theories and principles to study entities within specific domains. For example, [social ontology](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Social_ontology "Social ontology") examines basic concepts used in the [social sciences](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Social_sciences "Social sciences"). Applied ontology is particularly relevant to [information](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Information_science "Information science") and [computer science](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Computer_science "Computer science"), which develop [conceptual frameworks of limited domains](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ontology_(information_science) "Ontology (information science)"). These frameworks facilitate the structured storage of information, such as in a college database tracking academic activities. Ontology is also pertinent to the fields of [logic](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Logic "Logic"), [theology](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Theology "Theology"), and [anthropology](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Anthropology "Anthropology").

The [origins of ontology](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_ontology "History of ontology") lie in the [ancient period](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ancient_history "Ancient history") with speculations about the nature of being and the source of the universe, including ancient [Indian](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Indian_philosophy "Indian philosophy"), [Chinese](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Chinese_philosophy "Chinese philosophy"), and [Greek philosophy](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ancient_Greek_philosophy "Ancient Greek philosophy"). In the modern period, philosophers conceived ontology as a distinct academic discipline and coined its name.

Ontology is the study of being. It is the branch of [philosophy](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Philosophy "Philosophy") that investigates the nature of [existence](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Existence "Existence"), the features all entities have in common, and how they are divided into basic [categories of being](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Categories_of_being "Categories of being").[[1]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ontology#cite_note-1) It aims to discover the foundational building blocks of the world and characterize [reality](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Reality "Reality") as a whole in its most general aspects.[[a]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ontology#cite_note-3) In this regard, ontology contrasts with individual sciences like [biology](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Biology "Biology") and [astronomy](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Astronomy "Astronomy"), which restrict themselves to a limited domain of entities, such as living entities and celestial phenomena.[[3]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ontology#cite_note-4) In some contexts, the term _ontology_ refers not to the general study of being but to a specific ontological theory within this discipline. It can also mean an inventory or a [conceptual scheme](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Conceptual_scheme "Conceptual scheme") of a particular domain, such as [the ontology of genes](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gene_ontology "Gene ontology").[[4]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ontology#cite_note-5) In this context, an inventory is a comprehensive list of elements.[[5]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ontology#cite_note-6) A conceptual scheme is a framework of the key concepts and their relationships.[[6]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ontology#cite_note-7)

Ontology is closely related to [metaphysics](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Metaphysics "Metaphysics") but the exact relation of these two disciplines is disputed. A traditionally influential characterization asserts that ontology is a subdiscipline of metaphysics. According to this view, metaphysics is the study of various aspects of fundamental reality, whereas ontology restricts itself to the most general features of reality.[[7]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ontology#cite_note-8) This view sees ontology as general metaphysics, which is to be distinguished from special metaphysics focused on more specific subject matters, like [God](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/God "God"), [mind](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mind "Mind"), and [value](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Value_(ethics_and_social_sciences) "Value (ethics and social sciences)").[[8]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ontology#cite_note-9) A different conception understands ontology as a preliminary discipline that provides a complete inventory of reality while metaphysics examines the features and structure of the entities in this inventory.[[9]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ontology#cite_note-10) Another conception says that metaphysics is about real being while ontology examines possible being or the concept of being.[[10]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ontology#cite_note-11) It is not universally accepted that there is a clear boundary between metaphysics and ontology. Some philosophers use both terms as synonyms.[[11]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ontology#cite_note-12)

The etymology of the word _ontology_ traces back to the [ancient Greek](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ancient_Greek "Ancient Greek") terms ὄντως (_ontos_, meaning 'being') and λογία (_logia_, meaning 'study of'), literally, 'the study of being'. The ancient Greeks did not use the term _ontology_, which was coined by philosophers in the 17th century.[[12]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ontology#cite_note-13)

[![Image 1: Photo of a chair](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/04/Chair_2020_P01.jpg/120px-Chair_2020_P01.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Chair_2020_P01.jpg)

[![Image 2: Photo of a dog](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dc/Canis_lupus_familiaris_Perro_Mestizo.JPG/250px-Canis_lupus_familiaris_Perro_Mestizo.JPG)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Canis_lupus_familiaris_Perro_Mestizo.JPG)

[![Image 3: Photo of the Moon](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dd/Full_Moon_Luc_Viatour.jpg/250px-Full_Moon_Luc_Viatour.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Full_Moon_Luc_Viatour.jpg)

[![Image 4: Abstract depiction of ideas](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/72/Ideas.svg/120px-Ideas.svg.png)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Ideas.svg)

[![Image 5: Number 7](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ef/Number_7.svg/120px-Number_7.svg.png)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Number_7.svg)

[![Image 6: Drawing of a dragon](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d8/Friedrich-Johann-Justin-Bertuch_Mythical-Creature-Dragon_1806.jpg/250px-Friedrich-Johann-Justin-Bertuch_Mythical-Creature-Dragon_1806.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Friedrich-Johann-Justin-Bertuch_Mythical-Creature-Dragon_1806.jpg)

The scope of ontology covers diverse entities, including everyday objects, living beings, celestial bodies, ideas, numbers, and fictional creatures.

Being, or [existence](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Existence "Existence"), is the main topic of ontology. It is one of the most general and fundamental concepts, encompassing all of [reality](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Reality "Reality") and every [entity](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Entity "Entity") within it.[[b]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ontology#cite_note-15) In its broadest sense, being only contrasts with non-being or nothingness.[[14]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ontology#cite_note-16) It is controversial whether a more substantial analysis of the concept or meaning of being is possible.[[15]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ontology#cite_note-17) One proposal understands being as a property possessed by every entity.[[16]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ontology#cite_note-18) Critics argue that a thing without being cannot have properties. This means that properties presuppose being and cannot explain it.[[17]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ontology#cite_note-19) Another suggestion is that all beings share a set of essential features. According to the **Eleatic principle**, "power is the mark of being", meaning that only entities with [causal](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Causality "Causality") influence truly exist.[[18]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ontology#cite_note-20) A controversial proposal by philosopher [George Berkeley](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/George_Berkeley "George Berkeley") suggests that all existence is mental. He expressed this [immaterialism](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Immaterialism "Immaterialism") in his slogan "to be is to be perceived".[[19]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ontology#cite_note-21)

Depending on the context, the term _being_ is sometimes used with a more limited meaning to refer only to certain aspects of reality. In one sense, being is unchanging and permanent, in contrast to becoming, which implies change.[[20]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ontology#cite_note-22) Another contrast is between being, as what truly exists, and [phenomena](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Phenomenon "Phenomenon"), as what appears to exist.[[21]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ontology#cite_note-23) In some contexts, being expresses the fact that something is while [essence](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Essence "Essence") expresses its [qualities](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Quality_(philosophy) "Quality (philosophy)") or what it is like.[[22]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ontology#cite_note-24)

Ontologists often divide being into fundamental classes or highest kinds, called _[categories of being](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Categories\_of\_being "Categories of being")_.[[23]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ontology#cite_note-Thomasson2022-25) Proposed categories include substance, [property](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Property_(philosophy) "Property (philosophy)"), [relation](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Relation_(philosophy) "Relation (philosophy)"), [state of affairs](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/State_of_affairs_(philosophy) "State of affairs (philosophy)"), and [event](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Event_(philosophy) "Event (philosophy)").[[24]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ontology#cite_note-26) They can be used to provide systems of categories, which offer a comprehensive inventory of reality in which every entity belongs to exactly one category.[[23]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ontology#cite_note-Thomasson2022-25) Some philosophers, like [Aristotle](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Aristotle "Aristotle"), say that entities belonging to different categories exist in distinct ways. Others, like [John Duns Scotus](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/John_Duns_Scotus "John Duns Scotus"), insist that there are no differences in the mode of being, meaning that [everything exists in the same way](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Univocity_of_being "Univocity of being").[[25]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ontology#cite_note-27) A related dispute is whether some entities have a higher degree of being than others, an idea already found in [Plato](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Plato "Plato")'s work. The more common view in contemporary philosophy is that a thing either exists or not with no intermediary states or degrees.[[26]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ontology#cite_note-28)

The relation between being and non-being is a frequent topic in ontology. Influential issues include the status of [nonexistent objects](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nonexistent_objects "Nonexistent objects")[[27]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ontology#cite_note-29) and [why there is something rather than nothing](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Why_is_there_anything_at_all%3F "Why is there anything at all?").[[28]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ontology#cite_note-30)

### Particulars and universals

[[edit](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Ontology&action=edit&section=4 "Edit section: Particulars and universals")]

[![Image 7: Photo of the Taj Mahal](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1d/Taj_Mahal_%28Edited%29.jpeg/330px-Taj_Mahal_%28Edited%29.jpeg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Taj_Mahal "Taj Mahal")

[![Image 8: Patch of green](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3e/Color_green.svg/120px-Color_green.svg.png)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Green "Green")

The [Taj Mahal](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Taj_Mahal "Taj Mahal") is a particular entity while the color _green_ is a universal entity.

A central distinction in ontology is between particular and universal entities. Particulars, also called _individuals_, are unique, non-repeatable entities, like [Socrates](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Socrates "Socrates"), the [Taj Mahal](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Taj_Mahal "Taj Mahal"), and [Mars](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mars "Mars").[[29]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ontology#cite_note-31) Universals are general, repeatable entities, like the color _green_, the form _circularity_, and the virtue _courage_. Universals express aspects or features shared by particulars. For example, [Mount Everest](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mount_Everest "Mount Everest") and [Mount Fuji](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mount_Fuji "Mount Fuji") are particulars characterized by the universal _mountain_.[[30]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ontology#cite_note-32)

Universals can take the form of properties or relations.[[31]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ontology#cite_note-33)[[c]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ontology#cite_note-35) Properties describe the characteristics of things. They are features or qualities possessed by an entity.[[33]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ontology#cite_note-36) Properties are often divided into [essential and accidental properties](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Property_(philosophy)#Essential_and_accidental "Property (philosophy)"). A property is essential if an entity must have it; it is accidental if the entity can exist without it.[[34]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ontology#cite_note-37) For instance, _having three sides_ is an essential property of a triangle, whereas _being red_ is an accidental property.[[35]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ontology#cite_note-38)[[d]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ontology#cite_note-40) Relations are ways how two or more entities stand to one another. Unlike properties, they apply to several entities and characterize them as a group.[[37]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ontology#cite_note-41) For example, _being a city_ is a property while _being east of_ is a relation, as in "[Kathmandu](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kathmandu "Kathmandu") is a city" and "Kathmandu is east of [New Delhi](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/New_Delhi "New Delhi")".[[38]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ontology#cite_note-42) Relations are often divided into [internal and external relations](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Relation_(philosophy)#Internal_and_external "Relation (philosophy)"). Internal relations depend only on the properties of the objects they connect, like the relation of [resemblance](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Similarity_(philosophy) "Similarity (philosophy)"). External relations express characteristics that go beyond what the connected objects are like, such as spatial relations.[[39]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ontology#cite_note-43)

Substances[[e]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ontology#cite_note-45) play an important role in the [history of ontology](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_ontology "History of ontology") as the particular entities that underlie and support properties and relations. They are often considered the fundamental building blocks of reality that can exist on their own, while entities like properties and relations cannot exist without substances. Substances persist through changes as they acquire or lose properties. For example, when a tomato ripens, it loses the property _green_ and acquires the property _red_.[[41]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ontology#cite_note-46)

States of affairs are complex particular entities that have several other entities as their components. The state of affairs "Socrates is wise" has two components: the individual _Socrates_ and the property _wise_. States of affairs that correspond to reality are called _[facts](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Fact "Fact")_.[[42]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ontology#cite_note-47)[[f]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ontology#cite_note-49) Facts are [truthmakers](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Truthmaker_theory "Truthmaker theory") of statements, meaning that whether a statement is true or false depends on the underlying facts.[[44]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ontology#cite_note-50)

Events are particular entities[[g]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ontology#cite_note-52) that occur in time, like the [fall of the Berlin Wall](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Fall_of_the_Berlin_Wall "Fall of the Berlin Wall") and the [first moon landing](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/First_moon_landing "First moon landing"). They usually involve some kind of change, like the lawn becoming dry. In some cases, no change occurs, like the lawn staying wet.[[46]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ontology#cite_note-53) Complex events, also called _processes_, are composed of a sequence of events.[[47]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ontology#cite_note-54)

### Concrete and abstract objects

[[edit](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Ontology&action=edit&section=5 "Edit section: Concrete and abstract objects")]

Concrete objects are entities that exist in space and time, such as a tree, a car, and a planet. They have causal powers and can affect each other, like when a car hits a tree and both are deformed in the process. Abstract objects, by contrast, are outside space and time, such as the number 7 and the set of [integers](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Integer "Integer"). They lack causal powers and do not undergo changes.[[48]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ontology#cite_note-55)[[h]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ontology#cite_note-57) The existence and nature of abstract objects remain subjects of philosophical debate.[[50]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ontology#cite_note-58)

Concrete objects encountered in everyday life are complex entities composed of various parts. For example, a book is made up of two covers and the pages between them. Each of these components is itself constituted of smaller parts, like [molecules](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Molecule "Molecule"), [atoms](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Atom "Atom"), and [elementary particles](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Elementary_particle "Elementary particle").[[51]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ontology#cite_note-59)[Mereology](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mereology "Mereology") studies the relation between parts and wholes. One position in mereology says that every collection of entities forms a whole. According to another view, this is only the case for collections that fulfill certain requirements, for instance, that the entities in the collection touch one another.[[52]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ontology#cite_note-60) The problem of material constitution asks whether or in what sense a whole should be considered a new object in addition to the collection of parts composing it.[[53]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ontology#cite_note-61)

Abstract objects are closely related to fictional and [intentional objects](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Object_of_the_mind "Object of the mind"). Fictional objects are entities invented in works of [fiction](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Fiction "Fiction"). They can be things, like the [One Ring](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/One_Ring "One Ring") in [J. R. R. Tolkien](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/J._R._R._Tolkien "J. R. R. Tolkien")'s book series [The Lord of the Rings](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/The_Lord_of_the_Rings "The Lord of the Rings"), and people, like the [Monkey King](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Monkey_King "Monkey King") in the novel [Journey to the West](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Journey_to_the_West "Journey to the West").[[54]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ontology#cite_note-62) Some philosophers say that fictional objects are abstract objects and exist outside space and time. Others understand them as artifacts that are created as the works of fiction are written.[[55]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ontology#cite_note-63) Intentional objects are entities that exist within [mental states](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mental_states "Mental states"), like [perceptions](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Perception "Perception"), [beliefs](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Belief "Belief"), and [desires](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Desire "Desire"). For example, if a person thinks about the [Loch Ness Monster](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Loch_Ness_Monster "Loch Ness Monster") then the Loch Ness Monster is the intentional object of this [thought](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Thought "Thought"). People can think about existing and non-existing objects. This makes it difficult to assess the [ontological status of intentional objects](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Intentionality#The_problem_of_intentional_inexistence "Intentionality").[[56]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ontology#cite_note-64)

Ontological dependence is a relation between entities. An entity depends ontologically on another entity if the first entity cannot exist without the second entity.[[57]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ontology#cite_note-65) For instance, the surface of an apple cannot exist without the apple.[[58]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ontology#cite_note-66) An entity is ontologically independent if it does not depend on anything else, meaning that it is fundamental and can exist on its own. Ontological dependence plays a central role in ontology and its attempt to describe reality on its most fundamental level.[[59]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ontology#cite_note-67) It is closely related to [metaphysical grounding](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Grounding_(metaphysics) "Grounding (metaphysics)"), which is the relation between a ground and the facts it explains.[[60]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ontology#cite_note-68)

[![Image 9: Photo of Willard Van Orman Quine](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cd/Willard_Van_Orman_Quine_on_Bluenose_II_in_Halifax_NS_harbor_1980.jpg/250px-Willard_Van_Orman_Quine_on_Bluenose_II_in_Halifax_NS_harbor_1980.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Willard_Van_Orman_Quine_on_Bluenose_II_in_Halifax_NS_harbor_1980.jpg)

[Willard Van Orman Quine](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Willard_Van_Orman_Quine "Willard Van Orman Quine") used the concept of [ontological commitments](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ontological_commitment "Ontological commitment") to analyze theories.

An [ontological commitment](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ontological_commitment "Ontological commitment") of a person or a theory is an entity that exists according to them.[[61]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ontology#cite_note-69) For instance, a person who [believes in God](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Theism "Theism") has an ontological commitment to [God](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/God "God").[[62]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ontology#cite_note-70) Ontological commitments can be used to analyze which ontologies people explicitly defend or implicitly assume. They play a central role in contemporary metaphysics when trying to decide between competing theories. For example, the [Quine–Putnam indispensability argument](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Quine%E2%80%93Putnam_indispensability_argument "Quine–Putnam indispensability argument") defends [mathematical Platonism](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mathematical_Platonism "Mathematical Platonism"), asserting that numbers exist because the best scientific theories are ontologically committed to numbers.[[63]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ontology#cite_note-71)

Possibility and necessity are further topics in ontology. Possibility describes what can be the case, as in "it is possible that [extraterrestrial life](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Extraterrestrial_life "Extraterrestrial life") exists". Necessity describes what must be the case, as in "it is necessary that three plus two equals five". Possibility and necessity contrast with actuality, which describes what is the case, as in "[Doha](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Doha "Doha") is the capital of [Qatar](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Qatar "Qatar")". Ontologists often use the concept of [possible worlds](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Possible_world "Possible world") to analyze possibility and necessity.[[64]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ontology#cite_note-72) A possible world is a complete and consistent way how things could have been.[[65]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ontology#cite_note-73) For example, [Haruki Murakami](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Haruki_Murakami "Haruki Murakami") was born in 1949 in the actual world but there are possible worlds in which he was born at a different date. Using this idea, [possible world semantics](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Possible_world_semantics "Possible world semantics") says that a sentence is possibly true if it is true in at least one possible world. A sentence is necessarily true if it is true in all possible worlds.[[66]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ontology#cite_note-74) The field of [modal logic](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Modal_logic "Modal logic") provides a precise formalization of the concepts of possibility and necessity.[[67]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ontology#cite_note-75)

In ontology, [identity](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Identity_(philosophy) "Identity (philosophy)") means that two things are the same. Philosophers distinguish between qualitative and numerical identity. Two entities are qualitatively identical if they have exactly the same features, such as perfect identical twins. This is also called _exact similarity_ and _indiscernibility_. Numerical identity, by contrast, means that there is only a single entity. For example, if Fatima is the mother of Leila and Hugo then Leila's mother is numerically identical to Hugo's mother.[[68]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ontology#cite_note-76) Another distinction is between synchronic and diachronic identity. Synchronic identity relates an entity to itself at the same time. Diachronic identity relates an entity to itself at different times, as in "the woman who bore Leila three years ago is the same woman who bore Hugo this year".[[69]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ontology#cite_note-77) The notion of identity also has a number of philosophical implications in terms of how it interacts with the aforementioned necessity and possibility. Most famously, Saul Kripke contended that _discovered identities_ such as "Water is H 2 O" are necessarily true because "H 2 O" is what's known as a [rigid designator](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Rigid_designator "Rigid designator"). [[70]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ontology#cite_note-78)

There are different and sometimes overlapping ways to divide ontology into branches. Pure ontology focuses on the most abstract topics associated with the concept and nature of being. It is not restricted to a specific domain of entities and studies existence and the structure of reality as a whole.[[71]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ontology#cite_note-79) Pure ontology contrasts with [applied ontology](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Applied_ontology "Applied ontology"), also called domain ontology. Applied ontology examines the application of ontological theories and principles to specific disciplines and domains, often in the field of science.[[72]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ontology#cite_note-80) It considers ontological problems in regard to specific entities such as [matter](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Matter "Matter"), [mind](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mind "Mind"), [numbers](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Number "Number"), [God](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/God "God"), and cultural artifacts.[[73]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ontology#cite_note-81)

[Social ontology](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Social_ontology "Social ontology"), a major subfield of applied ontology, studies social kinds, like [money](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Money "Money"), [gender](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gender "Gender"), [society](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Society "Society"), and [language](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Language "Language"). It aims to determine the nature and essential features of these concepts while also examining their mode of existence.[[74]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ontology#cite_note-82) According to a common view, social kinds are useful constructions to describe the complexities of social life. This means that they are not pure fictions but, at the same time, lack the objective or mind-independent reality of natural phenomena like elementary particles, lions, and stars.[[75]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ontology#cite_note-83) In the fields of [computer science](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Computer_science "Computer science"), [information science](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Information_science "Information science"), and [knowledge representation](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Knowledge_representation "Knowledge representation"), applied ontology is interested in the development of formal frameworks to encode and store information about a limited domain of entities in a structured way.[[76]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ontology#cite_note-84) A related application in [genetics](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Genetics "Genetics") is [Gene Ontology](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gene_Ontology "Gene Ontology"), which is a comprehensive framework for the standardized representation of gene-related information across species and databases.[[77]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ontology#cite_note-85)

[Formal ontology](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Formal_ontology "Formal ontology") is the study of objects in general while focusing on their abstract structures and features. It divides objects into different categories based on the forms they exemplify. Formal ontologists often rely on the tools of [formal logic](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Formal_logic "Formal logic") to express their findings in an abstract and general manner.[[78]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ontology#cite_note-86)[[i]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ontology#cite_note-88) Formal ontology contrasts with material ontology, which distinguishes between different areas of objects and examines the features characteristic of a specific area.[[80]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ontology#cite_note-89) Examples are ideal spatial beings in the area of geometry and living beings in the area of biology.[[81]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ontology#cite_note-90)

Descriptive ontology aims to articulate the conceptual scheme underlying how people ordinarily think about the world. Prescriptive ontology departs from common conceptions of the structure of reality and seeks to formulate a new and better conceptualization.[[82]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ontology#cite_note-91)

Another contrast is between analytic and speculative ontology. Analytic ontology examines the types and categories of being to determine what kinds of things could exist and what features they would have. Speculative ontology aims to determine which entities actually exist, for example, whether there are numbers or whether time is an illusion.[[83]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ontology#cite_note-92)

[![Image 10](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b7/Heidegger_3_%281960%29.jpg/250px-Heidegger_3_%281960%29.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Heidegger_3_(1960).jpg)

[Martin Heidegger](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Martin_Heidegger "Martin Heidegger") proposed fundamental ontology to study the meaning of being.

[Metaontology](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Metaontology "Metaontology") studies the underlying concepts, assumptions, and methods of ontology. Unlike other forms of ontology, it does not ask "what exists" but "what does it mean for something to exist" and "how can people determine what exists".[[84]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ontology#cite_note-93) It is closely related to [fundamental ontology](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Fundamental_ontology "Fundamental ontology"), an approach developed by philosopher [Martin Heidegger](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Martin_Heidegger "Martin Heidegger") that seeks to uncover the meaning of being.[[85]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ontology#cite_note-94)

### Realism and anti-realism

[[edit](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Ontology&action=edit&section=9 "Edit section: Realism and anti-realism")]

The term _realism_ is used for various theories[[j]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ontology#cite_note-96) that affirm that some kind of phenomenon is real or has mind-independent existence. Ontological realism is the view that there are [objective](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Subjectivity_and_objectivity_(philosophy) "Subjectivity and objectivity (philosophy)") facts about what exists and what the nature and categories of being are. Ontological realists do not make claims about what those facts are, for example, whether elementary particles exist. They merely state that there are mind-independent facts that determine which ontological theories are true.[[87]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ontology#cite_note-97) This idea is denied by ontological anti-realists, also called ontological deflationists, who say that there are no substantive facts one way or the other.[[88]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ontology#cite_note-98) According to philosopher [Rudolf Carnap](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Rudolf_Carnap "Rudolf Carnap"), for example, ontological statements are relative to language and depend on the ontological framework of the speaker. This means that there are no framework-independent ontological facts since different frameworks provide different views while there is no objectively right or wrong framework.[[89]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ontology#cite_note-99)

[![Image 11: Fresco showing Plato and Aristotle](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/98/Sanzio_01_Plato_Aristotle.jpg/250px-Sanzio_01_Plato_Aristotle.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Sanzio_01_Plato_Aristotle.jpg)

[Plato](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Plato "Plato") (left) and [Aristotle](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Aristotle "Aristotle") (right) disagreed on whether universals can exist without matter.

In a more narrow sense, realism refers to the existence of certain types of entities.[[90]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ontology#cite_note-100) Realists about universals say that universals have mind-independent existence. According to [Platonic realists](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Theory_of_forms "Theory of forms"), universals exist not only independent of the mind but also independent of particular objects that exemplify them. This means that the universal _red_ could exist by itself even if there were no red objects in the world. Aristotelian realism, also called [moderate realism](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Moderate_realism "Moderate realism"), rejects this idea and says that universals only exist as long as there are objects that exemplify them. [Conceptualism](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Conceptualism "Conceptualism"), by contrast, is a form of anti-realism, stating that universals only exist in the mind as concepts that people use to understand and categorize the world. [Nominalists](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nominalism "Nominalism") defend a strong form of anti-realism by saying that universals have no existence. This means that the world is entirely composed of particular objects.[[91]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ontology#cite_note-101)

[Mathematical realism](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mathematical_realism "Mathematical realism"), a closely related view in the [philosophy of mathematics](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Philosophy_of_mathematics "Philosophy of mathematics"), says that mathematical facts exist independently of human language, thought, and practices and are discovered rather than invented. According to mathematical Platonism, this is the case because of the existence of [mathematical objects](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mathematical_objects "Mathematical objects"), like numbers and sets. Mathematical Platonists say that mathematical objects are as real as physical objects, like atoms and stars, even though they are not accessible to [empirical observation](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Empirical_observation "Empirical observation").[[92]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ontology#cite_note-102) Influential forms of mathematical anti-realism include conventionalism, which says that mathematical theories are trivially true simply by how mathematical terms are defined, and game [formalism](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Formalism_(philosophy_of_mathematics) "Formalism (philosophy of mathematics)"), which understands mathematics not as a theory of reality but as a game governed by rules of string manipulation.[[93]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ontology#cite_note-103)

[Modal realism](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Modal_realism "Modal realism") is the theory that in addition to the actual world, there are countless [possible worlds](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Possible_world "Possible world") as real and concrete as the actual world. The primary difference is that the actual world is inhabited by us while other possible worlds are inhabited by our [counterparts](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Counterpart_theory "Counterpart theory"). Modal anti-realists reject this view and argue that possible worlds do not have concrete reality but exist in a different sense, for example, as abstract or fictional objects.[[94]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ontology#cite_note-104)

[Scientific realists](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Scientific_realism "Scientific realism") say that the scientific description of the world is an accurate representation of reality.[[k]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ontology#cite_note-106) It is of particular relevance in regard to things that [cannot be directly observed](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Unobservable "Unobservable") by humans but are assumed to exist by scientific theories, like electrons, forces, and laws of nature. [Scientific anti-realism](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Scientific_anti-realism "Scientific anti-realism") says that scientific theories are not descriptions of reality but [instruments](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Instrumentalism "Instrumentalism") to predict observations and the outcomes of experiments.[[96]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ontology#cite_note-107)

[Moral realists](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Moral_realism "Moral realism") claim that there exist mind-independent moral facts. According to them, there are objective principles that determine which behavior is morally right. [Moral anti-realists](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Moral_anti-realism "Moral anti-realism") either claim that moral principles are subjective and differ between persons and cultures, a position known as [moral relativism](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Moral_relativism "Moral relativism"), or outright deny the existence of moral facts, a view referred to as [moral nihilism](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Moral_nihilism "Moral nihilism").[[97]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ontology#cite_note-108)

### By number of categories

[[edit](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Ontology&action=edit&section=10 "Edit section: By number of categories")]

Monocategorical theories say that there is only one fundamental category, meaning that every single entity belongs to the same universal class.[[98]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ontology#cite_note-109) For example, some forms of nominalism state that only concrete particulars exist while some forms of [bundle theory](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bundle_theory "Bundle theory") state that only properties exist.[[99]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ontology#cite_note-110) Polycategorical theories, by contrast, hold that there is more than one basic category, meaning that entities are divided into two or more fundamental classes. They take the form of systems of categories, which list the highest genera of being to provide a comprehensive inventory of everything.[[100]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ontology#cite_note-111)

The closely related discussion between [monism](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Monism "Monism") and [dualism](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mind%E2%80%93body_dualism "Mind–body dualism") is about the most fundamental types that make up reality. According to monism, there is only one kind of thing or substance on the most basic level.[[101]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ontology#cite_note-112)[Materialism](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Materialism "Materialism") is an influential monist view; it says that everything is material. This means that mental phenomena, such as beliefs, emotions, and consciousness, either do not exist or exist as aspects of matter, like brain states. [Idealists](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Idealists "Idealists") take the converse perspective, arguing that everything is mental. They may understand physical phenomena, like rocks, trees, and planets, as ideas or perceptions of conscious minds.[[102]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ontology#cite_note-113)[Neutral monism](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Neutral_monism "Neutral monism") occupies a middle ground by saying that both mind and matter are derivative phenomena.[[103]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ontology#cite_note-114) Dualists state that mind and matter exist as independent principles, either as [distinct substances](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Substance_dualism "Substance dualism") or [different types of properties](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Property_dualism "Property dualism").[[104]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ontology#cite_note-115) In a slightly different sense, monism contrasts with [pluralism](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pluralism_(philosophy) "Pluralism (philosophy)") as a view not about the number of basic types but the number of entities. In this sense, monism is the controversial position that only a single all-encompassing entity exists in all of reality.[[l]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ontology#cite_note-117) Pluralism is more commonly accepted and says that several distinct entities exist.[[106]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ontology#cite_note-118)

### By fundamental categories

[[edit](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Ontology&action=edit&section=11 "Edit section: By fundamental categories")]

The historically influential [substance-attribute ontology](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Substance_theory "Substance theory") is a polycategorical theory. It says that reality is at its most fundamental level made up of unanalyzable substances that are characterized by universals, such as the properties an individual substance has or relations that exist between substances.[[107]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ontology#cite_note-119) The closely related substratum theory says that each concrete object is made up of properties and a substratum. The difference is that the substratum is not characterized by properties: it is a featureless or _bare particular_ that merely supports the properties.[[108]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ontology#cite_note-120)

Various alternative ontological theories have been proposed that deny the role of substances as the foundational building blocks of reality.[[109]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ontology#cite_note-121) Stuff ontologies say that the world is not populated by distinct entities but by continuous stuff that fills space. This stuff may take various forms and is often conceived as infinitely divisible.[[110]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ontology#cite_note-122)[[m]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ontology#cite_note-124) According to [process ontology](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Process_philosophy "Process philosophy"), processes or events are the fundamental entities. This view usually emphasizes that nothing in reality is static, meaning that being is dynamic and characterized by constant change.[[112]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ontology#cite_note-125) Bundle theories state that there are no regular objects but only bundles of co-present properties. For example, a lemon may be understood as a bundle that includes the properties yellow, sour, and round. According to traditional bundle theory, the bundled properties are universals, meaning that the same property may belong to several different bundles. According to [trope](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Trope_(philosophy)#In_metaphysics "Trope (philosophy)") bundle theory, properties are particular entities that belong to a single bundle.[[113]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ontology#cite_note-126)

Some ontologies focus not on distinct objects but on interrelatedness. According to relationalism, all of reality is relational at its most fundamental level.[[114]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ontology#cite_note-127)[[n]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ontology#cite_note-129)[Ontic structural realism](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ontic_structural_realism "Ontic structural realism") agrees with this basic idea and focuses on how these relations form complex structures. Some structural realists state that there is nothing but relations, meaning that individual objects do not exist. Others say that individual objects exist but depend on the structures in which they participate.[[116]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ontology#cite_note-130) Fact ontologies present a different approach by focusing on how entities belonging to different categories come together to constitute the world. Facts, also known as states of affairs, are complex entities; for example, the fact that _the Earth is a planet_ consists of the particular object _the Earth_ and the property _being a planet_. Fact ontologies state that facts are the fundamental constituents of reality, meaning that objects, properties, and relations cannot exist on their own and only form part of reality to the extent that they participate in facts.[[117]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ontology#cite_note-131)[[o]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ontology#cite_note-133)

In the [history of philosophy](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_philosophy "History of philosophy"), various ontological theories based on several fundamental categories have been proposed. One of the first theories of categories was suggested by [Aristotle](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Aristotle "Aristotle"), whose system includes ten categories: substance, [quantity](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Quantity "Quantity"), [quality](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Quality_(philosophy) "Quality (philosophy)"), relation, place, date, posture, state, action, and passion.[[119]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ontology#cite_note-134) An early influential system of categories in Indian philosophy, first proposed in the [Vaisheshika](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Vaisheshika "Vaisheshika") school, distinguishes between six categories: [substance](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Dravya "Dravya"), quality, motion, universal, individuator, and inherence.[[120]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ontology#cite_note-135)[Immanuel Kant](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Immanuel_Kant "Immanuel Kant")'s [transcendental idealism](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Transcendental_idealism "Transcendental idealism") includes a system of twelve categories, which Kant saw as pure concepts of understanding. They are subdivided into four classes: quantity, quality, relation, and modality.[[121]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ontology#cite_note-136) In more recent philosophy, theories of categories were developed by [C. S. Peirce](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/C._S._Peirce "C. S. Peirce"), [Edmund Husserl](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Edmund_Husserl "Edmund Husserl"), [Samuel Alexander](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Samuel_Alexander "Samuel Alexander"), [Roderick Chisholm](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Roderick_Chisholm "Roderick Chisholm"), and [E. J. Lowe](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/E._J._Lowe_(philosopher) "E. J. Lowe (philosopher)").[[122]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ontology#cite_note-137)

The dispute between constituent and relational ontologies[[p]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ontology#cite_note-139) concerns the internal structure of concrete particular objects. Constituent ontologies say that objects have an internal structure with properties as their component parts. Bundle theories are an example of this position: they state that objects are bundles of properties. This view is rejected by relational ontologies, which say that objects have no internal structure, meaning that properties do not inhere in them but are externally related to them. According to one analogy, objects are like pin-cushions and properties are pins that can be stuck to objects and removed again without becoming a real part of objects. Relational ontologies are common in certain forms of nominalism that reject the existence of universal properties.[[124]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ontology#cite_note-140)

Hierarchical ontologies state that the world is organized into levels. Entities on all levels are real but low-level entities are more fundamental than high-level entities. This means that they can exist without high-level entities while high-level entities cannot exist without low-level entities.[[125]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ontology#cite_note-141) One hierarchical ontology says that elementary particles are more fundamental than the macroscopic objects they compose, like chairs and tables. Other hierarchical theories assert that substances are more fundamental than their properties and that nature is more fundamental than culture.[[126]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ontology#cite_note-142) Flat ontologies, by contrast, deny that any entity has a privileged status, meaning that all entities exist on the same level. For them, the main question is only whether something exists rather than identifying the level at which it exists.[[127]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ontology#cite_note-143)[[q]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ontology#cite_note-145)

The ontological theories of [endurantism](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Endurantism "Endurantism") and [perdurantism](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Perdurantism "Perdurantism") aim to explain how material objects persist through time. Endurantism is the view that material objects are three-dimensional entities that travel through time while being fully present in each moment. They remain the same even when they gain or lose properties as they change. Perdurantism is the view that material objects are four-dimensional entities that extend not just through space but also through time. This means that they are composed of [temporal parts](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Temporal_part "Temporal part") and, at any moment, only one part of them is present but not the others. According to perdurantists, change means that an earlier part exhibits different qualities than a later part. When a tree loses its leaves, for instance, there is an earlier temporal part with leaves and a later temporal part without leaves.[[129]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ontology#cite_note-146)

Differential ontology is a [poststructuralist](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Poststructuralist "Poststructuralist") approach interested in the relation between the concepts of identity and [difference](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Difference_(philosophy) "Difference (philosophy)"). It says that traditional ontology sees identity as the more basic term by first characterizing things in terms of their essential features and then elaborating differences based on this conception. Differential ontologists, by contrast, privilege difference and say that the identity of a thing is a secondary determination that depends on how this thing differs from other things.[[130]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ontology#cite_note-147)

[Object-oriented ontology](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Object-oriented_ontology "Object-oriented ontology") belongs to the school of [speculative realism](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Speculative_realism "Speculative realism") and examines the nature and role of objects. It sees objects as the fundamental building blocks of reality. As a flat ontology, it denies that some entities have a more fundamental form of existence than others. It uses this idea to argue that objects exist independently of human thought and perception.[[131]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ontology#cite_note-148)

[Methods](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Philosophical_methodology "Philosophical methodology") of ontology are ways of conducting ontological inquiry and deciding between competing theories. There is no single standard method; the diverse approaches are studied by [metaontology](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Metaontology "Metaontology").[[132]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ontology#cite_note-149)

[Conceptual analysis](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Conceptual_analysis "Conceptual analysis") is a method to understand ontological concepts and clarify their meaning.[[133]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ontology#cite_note-150) It proceeds by analyzing their component parts and the [necessary and sufficient conditions](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Necessary_and_sufficient_conditions "Necessary and sufficient conditions") under which a concept applies to an entity.[[134]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ontology#cite_note-151) This information can help ontologists decide whether a certain type of entity, such as numbers, exists.[[135]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ontology#cite_note-152)[Eidetic variation](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Eidetic_variation "Eidetic variation") is a related method in [phenomenological](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Phenomenology_(philosophy) "Phenomenology (philosophy)") ontology that aims to identify the essential features of different types of objects. Phenomenologists start by imagining an example of the investigated type. They proceed by varying the imagined features to determine which ones cannot be changed, meaning they are essential.[[136]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ontology#cite_note-153)[[r]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ontology#cite_note-155) The [transcendental](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Transcendental_arguments "Transcendental arguments") method begins with a simple observation that a certain entity exists. In the following step, it studies the ontological repercussions of this observation by examining how it is possible or which [conditions](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Condition_of_possibility "Condition of possibility") are required for this entity to exist.[[138]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ontology#cite_note-156)

Another approach is based on [intuitions](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Intuition "Intuition") in the form of non-inferential impressions about the correctness of general principles.[[139]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ontology#cite_note-157) These principles can be used as the [foundation](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Axiom "Axiom") on which an ontological system is built and expanded using [deductive reasoning](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Deductive_reasoning "Deductive reasoning").[[140]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ontology#cite_note-158) A further intuition-based method relies on [thought experiments](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Thought_experiment "Thought experiment") to evoke new intuitions. This happens by imagining a situation relevant to an ontological issue and then employing [counterfactual thinking](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Counterfactual_thinking "Counterfactual thinking") to assess the consequences of this situation.[[141]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ontology#cite_note-159) For example, some ontologists examine the relation between mind and matter by imagining [creatures identical to humans but without consciousness](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Philosophical_zombies "Philosophical zombies").[[142]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ontology#cite_note-160)

[Naturalistic methods](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Naturalism_(philosophy)#Methodological_naturalism "Naturalism (philosophy)") rely on the insights of the natural sciences to determine what exists.[[143]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ontology#cite_note-161) According to an influential approach by [Willard Van Orman Quine](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Willard_Van_Orman_Quine "Willard Van Orman Quine"), ontology can be conducted by analyzing[[s]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ontology#cite_note-163) the ontological commitments of scientific theories. This method is based on the idea that scientific theories provide the most reliable description of reality and that their power can be harnessed by investigating the ontological assumptions underlying them.[[145]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ontology#cite_note-164)

[![Image 12: Portrait of William of Ockham](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/20/Guillaume_Occam.jpg/250px-Guillaume_Occam.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Guillaume_Occam.jpg)

[William of Ockham](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/William_of_Ockham "William of Ockham") proposed [Ockham's Razor](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ockham%27s_Razor "Ockham's Razor"), a principle to decide between competing theories.

Principles of theory choice offer guidelines for assessing the advantages and disadvantages of ontological theories rather than guiding their construction.[[146]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ontology#cite_note-165) The principle of [Ockham's Razor](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ockham%27s_Razor "Ockham's Razor") says that simple theories are preferable.[[147]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ontology#cite_note-166) A theory can be simple in different respects, for example, by using very few basic types or by describing the world with a small number of fundamental entities.[[148]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ontology#cite_note-167) Ontologists are also interested in the explanatory power of theories and give preference to theories that can explain many observations.[[149]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ontology#cite_note-168) A further factor is how close a theory is to [common sense](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Common_sense "Common sense"). Some ontologists use this principle as an argument against theories that are very different from how ordinary people think about the issue.[[150]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ontology#cite_note-169)

In applied ontology, [ontological engineering](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ontological_engineering "Ontological engineering") is the process of creating and refining conceptual models of specific domains.[[151]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ontology#cite_note-170) Developing a new ontology from scratch involves various preparatory steps, such as delineating the scope of the domain one intends to model and specifying the purpose and use cases of the ontology. Once the foundational concepts within the area have been identified, ontology engineers proceed by defining them and characterizing the relations between them. This is usually done in a [formal language](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Formal_language "Formal language") to ensure precision and, in some cases, automatic [computability](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Computability "Computability"). In the following review phase, the validity of the ontology is assessed using test data.[[152]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ontology#cite_note-171) Various more specific instructions for how to carry out the different steps have been suggested. They include the [Cyc](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cyc "Cyc") method, Grüninger and Fox's methodology, and so-called METHONTOLOGY.[[153]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ontology#cite_note-172) In some cases, it is feasible to adapt a pre-existing ontology to fit a specific domain and purpose rather than creating a new one from scratch.[[154]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ontology#cite_note-173)

Ontology overlaps with many disciplines, including [logic](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Logic "Logic"), the study of [correct reasoning](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Logical_reasoning "Logical reasoning").[[155]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ontology#cite_note-174) Ontologists often employ [logical systems](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Formal_system "Formal system") to express their insights, specifically in the field of formal ontology. Of particular interest to them is the [existential quantifier](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Existential_quantifier "Existential quantifier") (![Image 13: {\displaystyle \exists }](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/77ed842b6b90b2fdd825320cf8e5265fa937b583)), which is used to express what exists. In [first-order logic](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/First-order_logic "First-order logic"), for example, the formula ![Image 14: {\displaystyle \exists x{\text{Dog}}(x)}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/f269feb75e01b3d220576f220fae6a2c0cf917e6) states that dogs exist.[[156]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ontology#cite_note-175) Some philosophers study ontology by examining the structure of thought and language, saying that they reflect the structure of being.[[157]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ontology#cite_note-176) Doubts about the accuracy of [natural language](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Natural_language "Natural language") have led some ontologists to seek a new [formal language](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Formal_language "Formal language"), termed _ontologese_, for a better representation of the fundamental structure of reality.[[158]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ontology#cite_note-177)

Suggested Upper Merged Ontology
Entity Physical Object Process Abstract Quantity Proposition Attribute Relation Set or Class
Fundamental categories in the [Suggested Upper Merged Ontology](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Suggested_Upper_Merged_Ontology "Suggested Upper Merged Ontology")[[159]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ontology#cite_note-178)

Ontologies are often used in information science to provide a conceptual scheme or inventory of a specific domain, making it possible to classify objects and formally represent information about them. This is of specific interest to computer science, which builds [databases](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Database "Database") to store this information and defines computational processes to automatically transform and use it.[[160]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ontology#cite_note-179) For instance, to encode and store information about clients and employees in a database, an organization may use an ontology with categories such as person, company, address, and name.[[161]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ontology#cite_note-180) In some cases, it is necessary to exchange information belonging to different domains or to integrate databases using distinct ontologies. This can be achieved with the help of [upper ontologies](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Upper_ontology "Upper ontology"), which are not limited to one specific domain. They use general categories that apply to most or all domains, like [Suggested Upper Merged Ontology](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Suggested_Upper_Merged_Ontology "Suggested Upper Merged Ontology") and [Basic Formal Ontology](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Basic_Formal_Ontology "Basic Formal Ontology").[[162]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ontology#cite_note-181)

Similar applications of ontology are found in various fields seeking to manage extensive information within a structured framework. [Protein Ontology](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Protein_Ontology "Protein Ontology") is a formal framework for the standardized representation of [protein](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Protein "Protein")-related entities and their relationships.[[163]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ontology#cite_note-182)[Gene Ontology](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gene_Ontology "Gene Ontology") and [Sequence Ontology](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sequence_Ontology "Sequence Ontology") serve a similar purpose in the field of [genetics](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Genetics "Genetics").[[164]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ontology#cite_note-183) Environment Ontology is a knowledge representation focused on [ecosystems](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ecosystems "Ecosystems") and environmental processes.[[165]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ontology#cite_note-184)[Friend of a Friend](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/FOAF "FOAF") provides a conceptual framework to represent relations between people and their interests and activities.[[166]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ontology#cite_note-185)

The topic of ontology has received increased attention in [anthropology](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Anthropology "Anthropology") since the 1990s, sometimes termed the "[ontological turn](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ontological_turn "Ontological turn")".[[167]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ontology#cite_note-186) This type of inquiry is focused on how people from different cultures experience and understand the nature of being. Specific interest has been given to the ontological outlook of [Indigenous people](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Indigenous_people "Indigenous people") and how it differs from a Western perspective.[[168]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ontology#cite_note-187) As an example of this contrast, it has been argued that various indigenous communities ascribe [intentionality](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Intentionality "Intentionality") to non-human entities, like plants, forests, or rivers. This outlook is known as [animism](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Animism "Animism")[[169]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ontology#cite_note-188) and is also found in [Native American](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Indigenous_peoples_of_the_Americas "Indigenous peoples of the Americas") ontologies, which emphasize the interconnectedness of all living entities and the importance of balance and harmony with nature.[[170]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ontology#cite_note-189)

Ontology is closely related to [theology](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Theology "Theology") and its interest in the [existence of God](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Existence_of_God "Existence of God") as an ultimate entity. The [ontological argument](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ontological_argument "Ontological argument"), first proposed by [Anselm of Canterbury](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Anselm_of_Canterbury "Anselm of Canterbury"), attempts to prove the existence of the divine. It defines [God](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/God "God") as the greatest conceivable being. From this definition it concludes that God must exist since God would not be the greatest conceivable being if God lacked existence.[[171]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ontology#cite_note-190) Another overlap in the two disciplines is found in ontological theories that use God or an ultimate being as the foundational principle of reality. Heidegger criticized this approach, terming it _[ontotheology](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ontotheology "Ontotheology")_.[[172]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ontology#cite_note-191)

[![Image 15: Depiction of Kapila](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/73/Kapila_%28cropped%29.png/250px-Kapila_%28cropped%29.png)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Kapila_(cropped).png)

[Kapila](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kapila "Kapila") was one of the founding fathers of the dualist school of [Samkhya](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Samkhya "Samkhya").[[173]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ontology#cite_note-192)

The roots of ontology in [ancient philosophy](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ancient_philosophy "Ancient philosophy") are speculations about the nature of being and the source of the universe. Discussions of the essence of reality are found in the [Upanishads](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Upanishads "Upanishads"), ancient Indian scriptures dating from as early as 700 BCE. They say that the universe has a divine foundation and discuss in what sense [ultimate reality](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Brahman "Brahman") is one or many.[[174]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ontology#cite_note-193)[Samkhya](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Samkhya "Samkhya"), the first [orthodox school of Indian philosophy](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/%C4%80stika_and_n%C4%81stika "Āstika and nāstika"),[[t]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ontology#cite_note-195) formulated an [atheist](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Atheist "Atheist") dualist ontology based on the Upanishads, identifying [pure consciousness](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Purusha "Purusha") and [matter](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Prakriti "Prakriti") as its two foundational principles.[[176]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ontology#cite_note-196) The later [Vaisheshika](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Vaisheshika "Vaisheshika") school[[u]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ontology#cite_note-198) proposed a comprehensive system of categories.[[178]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ontology#cite_note-199) In [ancient China](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ancient_China "Ancient China"), [Laozi](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Laozi "Laozi")'s (6th century BCE)[[v]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ontology#cite_note-201)[Taoism](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Taoism "Taoism") examines the underlying order of the universe, known as [Tao](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Tao "Tao"), and how this order is shaped by the interaction of two basic forces, [yin and yang](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Yin_and_yang "Yin and yang").[[180]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ontology#cite_note-202) The philosophical movement of [Xuanxue](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Xuanxue "Xuanxue") emerged in the 3rd century CE and explored the relation between being and non-being.[[181]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ontology#cite_note-203)

Starting in the 6th century BCE, [Presocratic philosophers](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Presocratic_philosophy "Presocratic philosophy") in [ancient Greece](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ancient_Greece "Ancient Greece") aimed to provide rational explanations of the universe. They suggested that a first principle, such as water or fire, is the primal source of all things.[[182]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ontology#cite_note-204)[Parmenides](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Parmenides "Parmenides") (c. 515–450 BCE) is sometimes considered the founder of ontology because of his explicit discussion of the concepts of being and non-being.[[183]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ontology#cite_note-205) Inspired by Presocratic philosophy, [Plato](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Plato "Plato") (427–347 BCE) developed his [theory of forms](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Theory_of_forms "Theory of forms"). It distinguishes between unchangeable perfect forms and matter, which has a lower degree of existence and imitates the forms.[[184]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ontology#cite_note-206)[Aristotle](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Aristotle "Aristotle") (384–322 BCE) suggested an elaborate system of categories that introduced the concept of substance as the primary kind of being.[[185]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ontology#cite_note-207) The school of [Neoplatonism](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Neoplatonism "Neoplatonism") arose in the 3rd century CE and proposed an ineffable source of everything, called _the One_, which is more basic than being itself.[[186]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ontology#cite_note-208)

The [problem of universals](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Problem_of_universals "Problem of universals") was an influential topic in medieval ontology. [Boethius](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Boethius "Boethius") (477–524 CE) suggested that universals can exist not only in matter but also in the mind. This view inspired [Peter Abelard](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Peter_Abelard "Peter Abelard") (1079–1142 CE), who proposed that universals exist only in the mind.[[187]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ontology#cite_note-209)[Thomas Aquinas](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Thomas_Aquinas "Thomas Aquinas") (1224–1274 CE) developed and refined fundamental ontological distinctions, such as the contrast between existence and [essence](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Essence "Essence"), between substance and accidents, and between [matter and form](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Matter_and_form "Matter and form").[[188]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ontology#cite_note-210) He also discussed the [transcendentals](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Transcendentals "Transcendentals"), which are the most general properties or modes of being.[[189]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ontology#cite_note-211)[John Duns Scotus](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/John_Duns_Scotus "John Duns Scotus") (1266–1308) argued that all entities, including God, [exist in the same way](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Univocity_of_being "Univocity of being") and that each entity has a unique essence, called [haecceity](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Haecceity "Haecceity").[[190]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ontology#cite_note-212)[William of Ockham](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/William_of_Ockham "William of Ockham") (c. 1287–1347 CE) proposed that one can decide between competing ontological theories by assessing which one uses the smallest number of elements, a principle known as [Ockham's razor](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ockham%27s_razor "Ockham's razor").[[191]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ontology#cite_note-213)

[![Image 16: Depiction of Zhu Xi](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9e/Zhu_xi.jpg/250px-Zhu_xi.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Zhu_xi.jpg)

The [Neo-Confucian](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Neo-Confucianism "Neo-Confucianism") philosopher [Zhu Xi](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Zhu_Xi "Zhu Xi") conceived the concept of [li](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Li_(neo-Confucianism) "Li (neo-Confucianism)") as the organizing principle of the universe.[[192]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ontology#cite_note-214)

In [Arabic-Persian philosophy](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Islamic_philosophy "Islamic philosophy"), [Avicenna](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Avicenna "Avicenna") (980–1037 CE) combined ontology with [theology](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Theology "Theology"). He identified God as a necessary being that is the source of everything else, which only has contingent existence.[[193]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ontology#cite_note-215) In 8th-century [Indian philosophy](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Indian_philosophy "Indian philosophy"), the school of [Advaita Vedanta](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Advaita_Vedanta "Advaita Vedanta") emerged.
