It says that only a single all-encompassing entity exists, stating that the impression of a plurality of distinct entities is an [illusion](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Maya_(religion) "Maya (religion)").[[194]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ontology#cite_note-216) Starting in the 13th century CE, the [Navya-Nyāya](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Navya-Ny%C4%81ya "Navya-Nyāya") school built on Vaisheshika ontology with a particular focus on the problem of non-existence and negation.[[195]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ontology#cite_note-217) 9th-century China saw the emergence of [Neo-Confucianism](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Neo-Confucianism "Neo-Confucianism"), which developed the idea that a rational principle, known as _[li](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Li\_(neo-Confucianism) "Li (neo-Confucianism)")_, is the ground of being and order of the cosmos.[[196]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ontology#cite_note-218)

[René Descartes](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ren%C3%A9_Descartes "René Descartes") (1596–1650) formulated a dualist ontology at the beginning of the modern period. It distinguishes between mind and matter as distinct substances that causally interact.[[197]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ontology#cite_note-219) Rejecting Descartes's dualism, [Baruch Spinoza](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Baruch_Spinoza "Baruch Spinoza") (1632–1677) proposed a monist ontology according to which there is only a single entity that is identical to [God and nature](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/God_or_Nature "God or Nature").[[198]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ontology#cite_note-220)[Gottfried Wilhelm Leibniz](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gottfried_Wilhelm_Leibniz "Gottfried Wilhelm Leibniz") (1646–1716), by contrast, said that the universe is made up of many simple substances, which are synchronized but do not interact with one another.[[199]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ontology#cite_note-221)[John Locke](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/John_Locke "John Locke") (1632–1704) proposed his substratum theory, which says that each object has a featureless substratum that supports the object's properties.[[200]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ontology#cite_note-222)[Christian Wolff](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Christian_Wolff_(philosopher) "Christian Wolff (philosopher)") (1679–1754) was influential in establishing ontology as a distinct discipline, delimiting its scope from other forms of metaphysical inquiry.[[201]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ontology#cite_note-223)[George Berkeley](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/George_Berkeley "George Berkeley") (1685–1753) developed an idealist ontology according to which material objects are ideas perceived by minds.[[202]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ontology#cite_note-224)

[Immanuel Kant](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Immanuel_Kant "Immanuel Kant") (1724–1804) rejected the idea that humans can have direct knowledge of independently existing things and their nature, limiting knowledge to the field of appearances. For Kant, ontology does not study external things but provides a system of [pure concepts of understanding](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Category_(Kant) "Category (Kant)").[[203]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ontology#cite_note-225) Influenced by Kant's philosophy, [Georg Wilhelm Friedrich Hegel](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Georg_Wilhelm_Friedrich_Hegel "Georg Wilhelm Friedrich Hegel") (1770–1831) linked ontology and [logic](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Logic "Logic"). He said that being and thought are identical and examined their foundational structures.[[204]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ontology#cite_note-226)[Arthur Schopenhauer](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Arthur_Schopenhauer "Arthur Schopenhauer") (1788–1860) rejected Hegel's philosophy and proposed that the world is an expression of a [blind and irrational will](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Voluntarism_(philosophy)#Metaphysical_voluntarism "Voluntarism (philosophy)").[[205]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ontology#cite_note-227)[Francis Herbert Bradley](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Francis_Herbert_Bradley "Francis Herbert Bradley") (1846–1924) saw absolute spirit as the ultimate and all-encompassing reality[[206]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ontology#cite_note-228) while denying that there are any external relations.[[207]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ontology#cite_note-229) In Indian philosophy, [Swami Vivekananda](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Swami_Vivekananda "Swami Vivekananda") (1863–1902) expanded on Advaita Vedanta, emphasizing the unity of all existence.[[208]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ontology#cite_note-230)[Sri Aurobindo](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sri_Aurobindo "Sri Aurobindo") (1872–1950) sought to understand the world as an evolutionary manifestation of a divine consciousness.[[209]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ontology#cite_note-231)

At the beginning of the 20th century, [Edmund Husserl](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Edmund_Husserl "Edmund Husserl") (1859–1938) developed [phenomenology](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Phenomenology_(philosophy) "Phenomenology (philosophy)") and employed its method, the description of [experience](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Experience "Experience"), to address ontological problems.[[210]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ontology#cite_note-232) This idea inspired his student [Martin Heidegger](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Martin_Heidegger "Martin Heidegger") (1889–1976) to clarify the meaning of being by exploring the mode of human existence.[[211]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ontology#cite_note-233)[Jean-Paul Sartre](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Jean-Paul_Sartre "Jean-Paul Sartre") responded to Heidegger's philosophy by examining the relation between being and [nothingness](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nothingness "Nothingness") from the perspective of human existence, freedom, and consciousness.[[212]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ontology#cite_note-234) Based on the phenomenological method, [Nicolai Hartmann](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nicolai_Hartmann "Nicolai Hartmann") (1882–1950) developed a complex hierarchical ontology that divides reality into four levels: inanimate, biological, psychological, and spiritual.[[213]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ontology#cite_note-235)

[![Image 17: Photo of Alexius Meinong](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/67/Alexius_Meinong_1900.jpg/250px-Alexius_Meinong_1900.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Alexius_Meinong_1900.jpg)

[Alexius Meinong](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Alexius_Meinong "Alexius Meinong") proposed that there are [nonexistent objects](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nonexistent_objects "Nonexistent objects").

[Alexius Meinong](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Alexius_Meinong "Alexius Meinong") (1853–1920) articulated a controversial ontological theory that includes nonexistent objects as part of being.[[214]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ontology#cite_note-236) Arguing against this theory, [Bertrand Russell](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bertrand_Russell "Bertrand Russell") (1872–1970) formulated a fact ontology known as [logical atomism](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Logical_atomism "Logical atomism"). This idea was further refined by the early [Ludwig Wittgenstein](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ludwig_Wittgenstein "Ludwig Wittgenstein") (1889–1951) and inspired [D. M. Armstrong](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/D._M._Armstrong "D. M. Armstrong")'s (1926–2014) ontology.[[215]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ontology#cite_note-237)[Alfred North Whitehead](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Alfred_North_Whitehead "Alfred North Whitehead") (1861–1947), by contrast, developed a process ontology.[[216]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ontology#cite_note-238)[Rudolf Carnap](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Rudolf_Carnap "Rudolf Carnap") (1891–1970) questioned the objectivity of ontological theories by claiming that what exists depends on one's linguistic framework.[[217]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ontology#cite_note-239) He had a strong influence on [Willard Van Orman Quine](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Willard_Van_Orman_Quine "Willard Van Orman Quine") (1908–2000), who analyzed the ontological commitments of scientific theories to solve ontological problems.[[218]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ontology#cite_note-240) Quine's student [David Lewis](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/David_Lewis_(philosopher) "David Lewis (philosopher)") (1941–2001) formulated the position of modal realism, which says that possible worlds are as real and concrete as the actual world.[[219]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ontology#cite_note-241) Since the end of the 20th century, interest in applied ontology has risen in computer and information science with the development of conceptual frameworks for specific domains.[[220]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ontology#cite_note-242)

*   [Hauntology](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hauntology "Hauntology")– Return or persistence of past ideas

1.   **[^](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ontology#cite_ref-3)**This focus on general principles rather than specific entities is traditionally expressed in the characterization of ontology as the science of _being qua being_ or _being insofar as it is being_.[[2]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ontology#cite_note-2)
2.   **[^](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ontology#cite_ref-15)**When used as a countable noun, _a being_ is the same as _an entity_.[[13]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ontology#cite_note-14)
3.   **[^](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ontology#cite_ref-35)**This idea is opposed by trope theorists, who understand properties and relations as particular entities.[[32]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ontology#cite_note-34)
4.   **[^](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ontology#cite_ref-40)**Other influential distinctions are between intrinsic and extrinsic properties, between determinate or determinable properties, and between categorical and dispositional properties.[[36]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ontology#cite_note-39)
5.   **[^](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ontology#cite_ref-45)**The term _substance_ has a specific meaning in philosophy distinct from ordinary language expressions such as _chemical substance_ or _substance abuse_.[[40]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ontology#cite_note-44)
6.   **[^](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ontology#cite_ref-49)**David Armstrong and his followers use a different terminology that does not distinguish between states of affairs and facts.[[43]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ontology#cite_note-48)
7.   **[^](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ontology#cite_ref-52)**Some ontologists also use the term in a less common sense to refer to universals in the form of event types.[[45]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ontology#cite_note-51)
8.   **[^](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ontology#cite_ref-57)**The precise definition is disputed.[[49]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ontology#cite_note-56)
9.   **[^](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ontology#cite_ref-88)**The idea of formal ontology was first formulated by phenomenologist [Edmund Husserl](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Edmund_Husserl "Edmund Husserl"), who studied objects in general by relying on fundamental categories such as unity, plurality, state of affairs, part, and whole. He examined the relations between these categories and how they depend on one another.[[79]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ontology#cite_note-87)
10.   **[^](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ontology#cite_ref-96)**They are usually distinguished by combining them with a qualifier to express which type is meant, as in _ontological realism_, _mathematical realism_, and _moral realism_. The qualifiers are sometimes left out if the meaning is clear in the context.[[86]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ontology#cite_note-95)
11.   **[^](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ontology#cite_ref-106)**The exact definition of the term is disputed.[[95]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ontology#cite_note-105)
12.   **[^](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ontology#cite_ref-117)**According to some [pantheists](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pantheism "Pantheism"), this entity is God.[[105]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ontology#cite_note-116)
13.   **[^](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ontology#cite_ref-124)**This view contrasts with atomism, which states that the world is composed of discrete, indivisible units.[[111]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ontology#cite_note-123)
14.   **[^](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ontology#cite_ref-129)**For example, [relationalism about spacetime](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Relationalism#Relationalism_(theory_of_space_and_time) "Relationalism") says that space and time are nothing but relations. Spacetime substantivalists reject this view and state that spacetime is a distinct object rather than a relational structure between objects.[[115]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ontology#cite_note-128)
15.   **[^](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ontology#cite_ref-133)**This is expressed in a slogan by [Ludwig Wittgenstein](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ludwig_Wittgenstein "Ludwig Wittgenstein"): "The world is the totality of facts, not of things".[[118]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ontology#cite_note-132)
16.   **[^](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ontology#cite_ref-139)**In this context, the term "relational ontology" has a slightly different meaning than the term "relationalism", which says that, at the most basic level, reality is made up of relations.[[123]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ontology#cite_note-138)
17.   **[^](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ontology#cite_ref-145)**Some flat ontologies allow that there are entities on higher levels but stipulate that they are reducible to the lowest level, meaning that they are no addition to being.[[128]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ontology#cite_note-144)
18.   **[^](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ontology#cite_ref-155)**For example, it is essential for a triangle to have three sides since it ceases to be a triangle if a fourth side is added.[[137]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ontology#cite_note-154)
19.   **[^](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ontology#cite_ref-163)**An essential step in Quine's analysis is to translate the theory into [first-order logic](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/First-order_logic "First-order logic") to make its ontological assumptions explicit.[[144]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ontology#cite_note-162)
20.   **[^](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ontology#cite_ref-195)**Its initial ideas were developed in the 7th and 6th centuries BCE but it was not until 350 CE that it received its classical and systematic formulation.[[175]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ontology#cite_note-194)
21.   **[^](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ontology#cite_ref-198)**The founding text of the school was written 500–300 BCE and the first major commentary on it is dated 400 CE.[[177]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ontology#cite_note-197)
22.   **[^](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ontology#cite_ref-201)**The exact date is disputed and some theorists suggest a later date between the 4th and 3rd centuries BCE.[[179]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ontology#cite_note-200)

*   Ludwig, David; Weiskopf, Daniel A. (2019). ["Ethnoontology: Ways of World-building Across Cultures"](https://doi.org/10.1111%2Fphc3.12621). _Philosophy Compass_. **14** (9). [doi](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Doi_(identifier) "Doi (identifier)"):[10.1111/phc3.12621](https://doi.org/10.1111%2Fphc3.12621). [S2CID](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/S2CID_(identifier) "S2CID (identifier)")[199516840](https://api.semanticscholar.org/CorpusID:199516840). Consider the animism debate. Animists consider nonhuman entities (e.g., plants, forests, or rivers) as intentional actors (Harvey, 2005). There is substantial evidence that animism is a widespread metaphysical view. For example, the Nayaka people of South India consider not only certain animals but also stones, hills, cups, and knives to be devaru: beings that stand in active, quasi-social relationships with them (Bird-David, 1999). Devaru are aspects of a larger kin structure that incorporates potential "partners" in the nonhuman world. In addition to these ethnographic observations, there are intriguing cross-cultural similarities in animist ontologies. Indigenous communities around the world tend to be much more permissive in their ascription of intentionality than Western participants (Ojalehto, Douglas, & García, 2017).

*   Scott, Michael W. (2013). ["The Anthropology of Ontology (Religious Science?)"](https://www.jstor.org/stable/42001687). _The Journal of the Royal Anthropological Institute_. **19** (4): 859–872. [doi](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Doi_(identifier) "Doi (identifier)"):[10.1111/1467-9655.12067](https://doi.org/10.1111%2F1467-9655.12067). [JSTOR](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/JSTOR_(identifier) "JSTOR (identifier)")[42001687](https://www.jstor.org/stable/42001687). [Archived](https://web.archive.org/web/20220924082557/https://www.jstor.org/stable/42001687) from the original on 2022-09-24. Retrieved 2022-09-24. Since roughly the 1990s, a growing number of anthropologists have become interested in the study of ontology – the investigation and theorization of diverse experiences and understandings of the nature of being itself. This generally takes the form of ethnographic accounts of indigenous non-Western modes and models of being, presented in more or less explicit contrast with aspects of a Euro-American or modern ontology imputed to conventional anthropology.
